    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="8f310d0563f04fb8b2b98c21537811a1">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '34bfc51b-5505-471d-a23f-1f68c535bfef'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d206427185" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Anionic redox chemistry is now viewed as the effective paradigm of improving the capacity of layered oxide materials in Sodium-ion battery. In this study, O3-type layered oxide NaLi<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Ru<sub>0.59</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NLCR) with O redox ability is successfully synthesized via a facile solid-state synthesis method. By manipulating the calcinate atmosphere with air and argon (sort by NLCR-Air NLCR-Ar respectively), a large amount of O vacancy is introduced in the NLCR-Air cathode. NLCR-Air with sufficient O vacancy exhibited superior rate performance which showed 87.7% capacity retention after 1000 cycles at 20&nbsp;C. Both NLCR-Air and NLCR-Ar showed activation of O redox properties which is supported by the soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS). Nevertheless, the in-situ X-ray diffraction and sXAS studies disclosed the O vacancy can promote the reversible phase transition and effectively suppress the irreversible O redox upon cycling. These are further supported by theoretical study which suggested a fast kinetic of Na diffusion and less electron agglomeration around the O atom for NLCR-Air with O vacancy.The research proposed a modification strategy with extraordinary reversible O redox property within O3-type layered cathode and offered a novel insight into understanding the anionic redox mechanism thus provide guidance of material design advanced energy storage systems.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202316342-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion battery (SIB), which is viewed as the alternative of the prevailing Li-ion battery (LIB), has now reaching a vast and constant concern worldwide.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0001_R_d206427105e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Chem. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering the nature abundant of sodium resources and comparable chemical reaction property with lithium element, the low cost and the fast commercialization of SIB are expected. Yet, the cycle life together with the relatively low energy density hinders its application at this stage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0002_R_d206427105e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Joule Energy Environ. Sci.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To alleviate the gap between market requirements and electrochemical properties, triggering the anionic redox chemistry was proposed as one of the promising strategies in recent years for the compensation of the capacity deficiency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0003_R_d206427105e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Indeed, the activation of anionic species, especially O atoms, could effectively increase the capacity, however, the sluggish kinetics,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0004_R_d206427105e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Commun.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> fast capacity fading,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0005_R_d206427105e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Power Sources">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> irreversible cation migration<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0006_R_d206427105e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as well as the large voltage hysteresis significantly inhibit its application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0007_R_d206427105e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To overcome such drawbacks, various tactics were employed such as manipulating the cation ordering<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0008_R_d206427105e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nature">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and constructing superstructure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0009_R_d206427105e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Engl.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> high covalence doping,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0010_R_d206427105e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> controlling the cation migration pathway,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0011_R_d206427105e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> suppressing the irreversible cation migration<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0012_R_d206427105e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and so on.</p>
         
         <p>By comparing two close Na─Li─Mn─O compounds, Bruce et. al. suggested the ribbon superstructure could inhibits the hysteresis and facilitate the cyclability,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0008_R_d206427105e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nature">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Guo et. al. made one step forwards and constructed dual-honeycomb in the layered structure by Li─Mg co-doping. The Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Li<sub>1/7</sub>Mn<sub>5/14</sub>][Mg<sub>1/7</sub>Mn<sub>5/14</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> exhibited a record discharge capacity of 285.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with an 85.5% capacity retention after 50 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0009_R_d206427105e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Engl.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high covalence doping with Ir atoms was proposed by Zhang et al in the sodium-rich layered materials Na<sub>1.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Ir<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> which effectively suppressing the O<sub>2</sub> generated and stable the structure evolution upon cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0010_R_d206427105e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Kang and co-workers disclosed the oxygen redox was highly related with the cation migration process thus affecting the O redox stability. Using substantially advanced material characterization techniques, Kang et. al. revealed that the P3-Na<sub>0.6</sub>[Li<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.8</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> showed inner plane TM migration with the formation of π-bonding which could assist the stability of O redox whereas the P3-Na<sub>0.6</sub>[Li<sub>0.2</sub>Ti<sub>0.2</sub>Mn<sub>0.6</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> displayed the outer plane TM migration and exhibited σ-interacting with O─O dimers that destabilized the cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0011_R_d206427105e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, the research from Liu's group suggested the Li─Cu co-doping could format Cu-(O─O) bonding and effectively suppress the irreversible cation migration during the anionic redox process, thus greatly improved the cycling stability. The Na<sub>0.8</sub>Cu<sub>0.22</sub>Li<sub>0.08</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> showed 82% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 10&nbsp;C rate.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0012_R_d206427105e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the very recent work from Li’ group proposed novel insights into the modification strategy by Zn/Ti and K/Ti co-doping. Their research disclosed the Zn/Ti co-doping could introduce the O3-P3-OP2 phase transition with the bettter lattice accommodation thus leading to the superior cycling performance with 93% capacity retention after 3600 cycles. Whereas the K/Ti co-doping provided rigid pillars to alleviate the slab gliding which led to the ultrastable cycling performance after 2000 cycles in the pouch cell. This modification strategy can be also applied in the anionic redox system in order to provide a substantial structure upon cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0013_R_d206427105e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Mater. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Engl.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>It is worth noting that most of the modification strategies were focused on the cation atoms and seldom attention was paid on the anion species, that is, O atoms. Different from the SIB, the optimization of the O redox property engineer in the LIB was also including the regulation of O atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0014_R_d206427105e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Using carbon thermal reduction followed by heat treatment, Cho et. al successfully synthesized Li<sub>2</sub>MnO<sub>3-δ</sub> powder. Both specific capacities as well as the cycling life were successfully enhanced compared with Li<sub>2</sub>MnO<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0015_R_d206427105e653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ChemElectroChem">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Theoretical research performed by Sushko's group elucidated the relationship between the voltage profile and O vacancy in the LiNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>1.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode material and suggested the suppression of voltage hysteresis by forming the inherent O vacancy in the LiNi<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>1.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0016_R_d206427105e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, introducing inherent O vacancy and boosting reversible O redox in the SIB is expected.</p>
         
         <p>Delmas et. al. have proposed a classification scheme for layered oxides based on the coordination configuration of sodium ions within the MO<sub>6</sub> polyhedral and the stacking mode of oxygen atoms, including O3, O2, P3, and P2, with O standing for Octahedral and P denoted as Prismatic, whereas the numerical digits represent the minimum number of repeating units in the oxygen stacking sequence.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0017_R_d206427105e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Mater. Res. Bull.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with sodium-deficient P2-type compounds, O3-type compounds have a higher sodium content, which provides more recyclable sodium and results in higher initial Coulombic efficiency. This plays a crucial role in practical sodium-ion battery systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0018_R_d206427105e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Energy Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Whereas most of the SIB layered structure compounds with anionic redox properties exhibit P2 structure. Since the Na has large radium compared with Li, it is difficult for the Na atoms insert into the TM layered in the O3 structure and form the A─O─A (A: Li Na alkalis) configuration that proved to be the key factor to triggering the O redox. Recently, Tarascon and co-workers reported O3-NaLi<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> material as the pioneer of a non-sodium rich O3 type compound with excellent O redox property. Nevertheless, their synthesis method was complicated compared with traditional solid-state synthesis method. Thus, to unify the mechanical picture of anionic redox in sodium ion battery and make a better use of anionic chemistry toward wildly used, it is necessary to explore other O3-type cathode materials with O redox property and simple synthesis methods.</p>
         
         <p>In the present study, a novel compound of O3-NaLi<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Ru<sub>0.59</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with O redox ability was proposed. The feasible solid-state synthesis method with the adoption of different sintering atmospheres were applied to achieve both stoichiometric O3-NaLi<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Ru<sub>0.59</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and non-stoichiometric O3-NaLi<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Ru<sub>0.59</sub>O<sub>2-x</sub> with oxygen vacancy. The electrochemical research showed the O3-NaLi<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Ru<sub>0.59</sub>O<sub>2-x</sub> with oxygen vacancy displayed an ultralong cyclability, that is, 89.1% after 500 cycles at 10C rate compared with 75.2% of O3-NaLi<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Ru<sub>0.59</sub>O<sub>2</sub> without O vacancy. The activation of O redox was further confirmed by ex-situ soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS). The in-situ XRD research disclosed the reversible phase transition of O3-NaLi<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Ru<sub>0.59</sub>O<sub>2-x</sub> with oxygen vacancy that facilitate the stability of redox chemistry. An in-depth study with first principle calculation elucidates the existence of Na─O─Li configuration in the O3-NaLi<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Ru<sub>0.59</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and the O3-NaLi<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Ru<sub>0.59</sub>O<sub>2-x</sub> could successfully triggering the O redox ability. Meanwhile the existence of O vacancy reduced the Na-ion transition energy barriers dramatically which promotes the cyclability as well as the ratability. Our research proposes the novel insight of improving the electrochemical property of O redox chemistry with O3 type SIB cathode materials which could have a significant impact of material design toward commercial application.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202316342-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202316342-sec-0030-title">2.1 Material Characterization and Electrochemical Overview</h3>
            
            <p>Previously research disclosed that different calcinated atmosphere has a large impact of the final compound including the promotion of the existence of O vacancy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0019_R_d206427105e785" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Res.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Two samples were synthesized in different calcinated atmosphere were obtained based on the facile solid-state method. To distinct the sample sintered in the different atmosphere, we denoted them as argon atmosphere (NLCR-Ar) and air atmosphere (NLCR-Air). Taking NLCR-Air as an example, the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images revealed that the powder sample comprised plate-like particles with dimensions ranging from 5–10&nbsp;µm. Energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) measurement of the material showcased a uniform distribution of Na, Co, Ru, and O elements throughout the material (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns showed both compounds could be attributed to the orthorhombic symmetry of the R-3m space group, confirming the successfully synthesized of O3 phase (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0001">1a,b</a>). Rietveld Refinement (GSAS) was used as the implement to get the crystallographic details of the two materials, encompassing lattice parameters, site occupancy, and atomic position parameters, which were presented in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), based on XRD data. Specifically, the results demonstrated that the c-axis for NLCR-Ar was 15.899&nbsp;Å, while for NLCR-Air, it extended to 16.288&nbsp;Å. This disparity implied that NLCR-Air possesses a larger c-axis, indicating a better sodium ion transport pathway within the material. Besides, the I(003)/I(104) intensity ratio for NLCR-Ar was 1.12, which was lower than the I(003)/I(104) intensity ratio of 7.20 for NLCR-Air. According to the study by Yang et al,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0020_R_d206427105e806" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Energy Chem.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> this indicated a lower degree of cation mixing in NLCR-Air. The increase in the I(003)/I(104) ratio suggested an increased exposure of the (003) crystal plane, which implied the presence of more sodium ion transport channels in the NLCR-Air material, thereby improving the migration rate of sodium ions.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202316342-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b027425e-9811-4879-b764-77aa69a92212/adfm202316342-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b027425e-9811-4879-b764-77aa69a92212/adfm202316342-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e39e1f88-965b-481b-97f3-541faa7ad84c/adfm202316342-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b027425e-9811-4879-b764-77aa69a92212/adfm202316342-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202316342-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202316342" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structure characterization of NLCRO material. XRD pattern and Rietveld refinement of the as-synthesized a) NLCR-Ar b) NLCR-Air sample. c) EPR spectra of NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air sample at 2&nbsp;K temperature. TEM image of d) NLCR-Ar e) NLCR-Air sample.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To confirm the existence of oxygen vacancies, electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) tests were conducted under vacuum conditions at 2&nbsp;K. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0001">1c</a>, NLCR-Air displayed a distinct signal (g = 2.02812), attributed to oxygen vacancies, while NLCR-Ar exhibited a weaker signal peak (g = 2.06347). This unambiguously disclosed that NLCR-Air features a higher concentration of oxygen vacancies compared to NLCR-Ar. Detailed crystallographic characterizations of the two samples were further analyzed using high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM). As can be seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0001">1d,e</a>, the (003) lattice spacing for NLCR-Ar was determined to be 0.533&nbsp;nm, while for NLCR-Air, this spacing was 0.546&nbsp;nm. HRTEM observations offer compelling visual evidence of the expanded interlayer spacing in NLCR-Air. This can be ascribed to the enlargement of the Na─O bond as depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). This expansion can be attributed to the presence of oxygen vacancies, which reduce the negative charge of oxygen, weakening the attractive forces between oxygen and sodium layers and leading to an increased interlayer spacing within the sodium layers. This finding also suggested that NLCR-Air could provide a broader sodium ion migration pathway, thereby lowering the energy barrier for sodium ion migration within the material.</p>
            
            <p>To unveil the impact of oxygen vacancy on the electrochemical performance, we conducted a comprehensive electrochemical study on both materials. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0002">2a,b</a> presented the electrochemical data recorded for the two materials under a current density of 0.1&nbsp;C (1C = 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) within a voltage window of 1.5–4.4&nbsp;V. The initial charge capacity for NLCR-Ar was 203.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, while for NLCR-Air, it was 175.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Notably, for the 0.1&nbsp;C charge/discharge curves, three distinct plateaus were observed during the charging process. The first plateau occurred near 2.5&nbsp;V, corresponding to the oxidation of Ru<sup>4+</sup>/Ru<sup>5+</sup> followed by the second plateau appeared between 3.0–3.5&nbsp;V, which can be ascribed to the oxidation of Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>4+</sup>. When the charging voltage surpasses 4.0&nbsp;V, the third plateau emerges, corresponding to the oxidation reaction of oxygen. These could be further confirmed by the cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests. Taking the analysis of NLCR-Ar's CV curve as an illustrative example, the peak observed between 2.0–2.5, 3.0–3.5&nbsp;V, and the peak above 4.0&nbsp;V corresponded to the redox peak of Ru<sup>4+</sup>/Ru<sup>5+</sup>, Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>4+</sup>, and the oxygen respectively. A comparative assessment of the CV curves between NLCR-Ar clearly indicated a more substantial contribution of the oxygen redox peak in NLCR-Ar, characterized by a sharper peak shape. This phenomenon suggests a heightened oxygen activity within the NLCR-Ar system thus contributing to the increasing of capacity. Therefore, the slightly larger first charge capacity of NLCR-Ar compared to NLCR-Air can be attributed to the activation of a larger amount of oxygen by NLCR-Ar, which also resulted in a larger discharge capacity subsequently. Despite the NLCR-Ar exhibiting larger capacity, the cycling stability which is related to the reversibility of the O redox process needs to be further discussed. The cycling tests were performed at different charging rates including 5, 10, and 20&nbsp;C. The results can be found in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a,b</a> (Supporting Information) respectively. For all the testing results, it was evident that NLCR-Air, with the abundant oxygen vacancies, displayed a higher capacity retention. This advantage became more pronounced with the increasing of current density. Specifically, at a charging current density of 5&nbsp;C, NLCR-Ar demonstrated a noticeable decline in capacity retention, with only 62% of its initial capacity after 500 cycles. In contrast, NLCR-Air showed remarkable cyclability, and presented an impressive 80.8% of its initial capacity. When the current density went to 10C, NLCR-Ar displayed a capacity retention of 75.2% after 500 cycles whereas for the NLCR-Air, it still managed to reach a capacity retention of 89.1% after 500 cycles, indicating its higher stability. Surprisingly, the O3-NLCR compound exhibited a super rate capability as well. For 20&nbsp;C tests, both NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air showed ≈110&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> capacity. The difference can be found in the cycling test in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0002">2b</a>. Our research disclosed that after 700 cycles, NLCR-Ar retained only 66.3% of its initial capacity. Yet, for the NLCR-Air, it reached capacity retention of 87.7% even after 1000 cycles. Moreover, the NLCR-Air sample could reach 91&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> even at 50&nbsp;C (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a,b</a>, Supporting Information). As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0002">2c</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), to the best of our knowledge, this NLCR-Air appeared the far better electrochemical property with ultra-long cycling performance and ultra-high rate ability compared with recently reported anionic redox layered oxide materials. This striking performance gap unveiled the significance of oxygen vacancies with the enhancement of cycling stability. The mechanism will be further discussed below. To explore the practical applicability of NLCR-Air, a full battery test was constructed employing the O3-NaLi<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Ru<sub>0.59</sub>O<sub>2-x</sub> and hard carbon as the cathode and anode part respectively. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the capacity retention was achieved as 95.9% after 200 cycles which indicated a potential application of NLCR-Air materials.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202316342-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c09bfc79-0b82-4e3b-ba15-76bddf2e8df2/adfm202316342-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c09bfc79-0b82-4e3b-ba15-76bddf2e8df2/adfm202316342-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e41c04a2-cf90-4fe4-b801-0df53f9bde0a/adfm202316342-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c09bfc79-0b82-4e3b-ba15-76bddf2e8df2/adfm202316342-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202316342-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202316342" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance analysis. a) The first cycle of the galvanostatic charge-discharge curve for NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air, conducted within a voltage range of 1.5–4.4&nbsp;V and at a rate of 0.1&nbsp;C. b) Cycling performance of NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air coin half cells, spanning 1.5–4.4&nbsp;V at a rate of 20&nbsp;C. c) Capacity retention statistics for some representative SIB cathodes. GITT potential profiles during discharge and charge processes for d) NLCR-Ar and e) NLCR-Air.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">Efforts to elucidate the distinct properties of NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air electrodes encompass the utilization of galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) and Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). To elucidate the distinct rate properties of NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air electrodes, the sodium ion diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) for both materials is determined via the GITT. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0002">2d,e</a> depicted the GITT curves for NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air during the first two charge-discharge cycles, utilizing a constant current density of 10&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> within a voltage range of 1.5–4.4&nbsp;V, with a pulse time of 30&nbsp;min and a relaxation time of 4&nbsp;h. The distinct charging and discharging steps along with voltage fluctuations during the relaxation process were observed clearly. The calculation of the D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> was based on the following Equation (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202316342-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>):

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202316342-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/30823dec-ddb9-430c-b90c-552505ac5d77/adfm202316342-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202316342:adfm202316342-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202316342-math-0001.png">

                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} {\mathrm{D}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^{+}}=\frac{4}{\pi \tau}\ {\left(\frac{{\mathrm{m}}_{\mathrm{B}}{\mathrm{V}}_{\mathrm{M}}}{{\mathrm{M}}_{\mathrm{B}}\mathrm{A}}\right)}^{2}{\left(\frac{\mathrm{\Delta}{\mathrm{E}}_{\mathrm{s}}}{\tau ({\mathrm{dE}}_{\tau}/\mathrm{d}\sqrt{\tau}}\right)}^{2},\quad \left(\tau \ll {\mathrm{L}}^{2}/{\mathrm{D}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}^{+}}\right) \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">When the constant current used was linearly related to the relaxation time, the above equation could be further simplified to the following Equation (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202316342-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>):

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202316342-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/39dfc84c-e3b3-4319-8a2d-1727302e2e4a/adfm202316342-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202316342:adfm202316342-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202316342-math-0002.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                                       <mo>+</mo>
                                    </msup>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mn>4</mn>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>π</mi>
                                    <mi>τ</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mfrac>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <msup>
                                 <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">V</mi>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mfenced>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                              <msup>
                                 <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                    <mfrac>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">E</mi>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi mathvariant="normal">E</mi>
                                             <mi>τ</mi>
                                          </msub>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfrac>
                                 </mfenced>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{{\mathrm{D}}}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}} = \frac{4}{{{\mathrm{\pi \tau }}}}\ {{\left( {\frac{{{{{\mathrm{m}}}_{\mathrm{B}}}{{{\mathrm{V}}}_{\mathrm{M}}}}}{{{{{\mathrm{M}}}_{\mathrm{B}}}{\mathrm{A}}}}} \right)}^2}{{\left( {\frac{{\Delta {{{\mathrm{E}}}_{\mathrm{s}}}}}{{\Delta {{{\mathrm{E}}}_{{\tau}}}}}} \right)}^2}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>In this formula, τ represented the time period of the current pulse under a specific current density; m<sub>B</sub>, MB, VM, A, and L represented the mass load, molecular weight, molar volume, electrode contact area, and thickness respectively; ΔEs and ΔEτ were two types of voltage differences. The D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of the first two cycles of NLCR-Ar was mainly between 10<sup>−12</sup> and 10<sup>−19</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, which was reduced by two orders of magnitude compared with 10<sup>−10</sup>–10<sup>−14</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> of NLCR-Air. Specifically, when the voltage below 4.0&nbsp;V the Ru<sup>4+</sup>/Ru<sup>5+</sup> and the Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>4+</sup> ions redox dominated the electrochemical reaction, the D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of both samples were relatively higher compared with the sluggish kinetic reaction when the O redox involved above 4.0&nbsp;V. This phenomenon was even pronounced for NLCR-Ar during the second cycle which implied the large structure change even an irreversible phase transition of NLCR-Ar upon cycling. For the NLCR-Air, despite the D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> was still lower in the high voltage region, the difference become smaller in the subsequent cycling. Also, the overall D<sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of NLCR-Air was at least two orders of magnitude larger than NLCR-Ar. Moreover, the corresponding EIS for NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air electrodes after different cycling numbers at a 5&nbsp;mV AC potential in a range of 0.01&nbsp;Hz and 0.1&nbsp;MHz were obtained. As can be seen in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information) after 50 cycles, the charge-transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) of the NLCR-Air sample with sufficient O vacancy was significantly smaller than that of the NLCR-Ar sample. Therefore, the results indicate that the incorporation of abundant oxygen vacancies can effectively reduce R<sub>ct</sub> and improve the electronic conductivity of NLCR-Air which was consistent with the previous research.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0019_R_d206427105e1159" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nano Res.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This suggested the existence of O vacancy could effectively promote the electronic conductivity as well as the Na diffusion thus improving the electrochemical performance.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202316342-sec-0040-title">2.2 Structure Evolution</h3>
            
            <p>In order to reveal the large impact of O vacancy upon cycling as mentioned above, in-depth investigations on the structure evolution were conducted using in situ X-ray Diffraction (in-situ XRD) techniques for both NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air respectively. As illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0003">3a,b</a>, during the extraction of sodium ions, corresponding to the initial charging phase, NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air exhibited analogous structural evolution. The (003) and (006) peaks of the O3 phase gradually shifted toward lower angles, indicating the expansion of c-lattice due to the reduction of O attraction from the residual Na atoms, which referred to the transformation from the O3 phase to the P3 phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0021_R_d206427105e1180" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the P3 phase maintained for a longer time for NLCR-Air with O vacancy compared with NLCR-Ar. The long last P3 phase constructed a prismatic interlayered structure in the sodium layer, therefore reducing the cation migration from the TM layered to the Na layered. The inhibition of cation migration effectively reduced the O redox ability therefore stabilizing the structure transformation during the subsequent cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0022_R_d206427105e1185" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Power Sources Chem. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With the continuous of sodium extraction, the (003) peak gradually moved toward higher angles around the O3 phase, accompanied by the vanish of the (006) peak at higher angles, characteristic of a phase with extremely low crystallinity. This phenomenon can reasonably be attributed to the formation of the novel phase with different space groups, resulting in the formation of the O’3 phase. The emergence of the O’3 phase predominated at the end of the charging process, and concurrently, the P3 phase diminished.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202316342-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f6d5ebe4-49b7-436f-9497-746549c2fb0b/adfm202316342-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f6d5ebe4-49b7-436f-9497-746549c2fb0b/adfm202316342-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/09936150-cd13-4c4c-8d56-e513270be3a9/adfm202316342-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f6d5ebe4-49b7-436f-9497-746549c2fb0b/adfm202316342-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202316342-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202316342" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">In-situ XRD patterns were collected during the initial cycle of a) NLCR-Ar and b) NLCR-Air electrodes under a 0.1&nbsp;C rate at voltage ranges of 1.5–4.4&nbsp;V.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>However, during the initial discharge cycle, the difference appeared on the structure evolution between NLCR-Air with abundant O vacancy and NLCR-Ar. For NLCR-Ar, in the early stages of discharge, the O’3 phase began to revert back to the P3 phase. This reversion persisted until the voltage dropped below 2.9&nbsp;V, at which point the O3 phase started to appear. After that, the coexistence of the P3 and O3 phases was observed, and this coexistence phase transition remained throughout the discharge process. In contrast, NLCR-Air maintained the P3 phase within the voltage range of 3.2–2.1&nbsp;V. As the discharge process continued, the sodium content within the layered oxide increased, and the (003) peak gradually shifted toward higher angles, reverting back to the O3 phase. The distinction between these two materials lies in NLCR-Ar's transformation from O3 to P3 during sodium extraction and reversed back to O'3, whereas, during sodium insertion, the P3 and O3 coexist in the NLCR-Ar. This can be ascribed to the lower phase transition energy barrier of NLCR-Air which was further discussed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). Similar to other O3 cathode materials, the irreversible phase transformation severely hinders the electrochemical performance, especially contributing to the lower cyclic performance of NLCR-Ar compared to NLCR-Air. Moreover, as can be seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0003">3b</a>, after discharging the (003) peak of NLCR-Air shifted toward to higher angle (≈16.78°) compared to the initial (003) state (≈16.39°). The slightly shifting with increasing broadness and lowered intensity of peaks indicating the material did not fully convert back to the pristine state which can be ascribed to the potential O<sub>2</sub> onset as reported by the previously research for the O3-type anionic redox-related materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0023_R_d206427105e1221" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nature J. Am. Chem. Soc. Energy Environ. Sci.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202316342-sec-0050-title">2.3 Charge Compensation and Oxygen Redox Evolution Process</h3>
            
            <p>To unveil the charge compensation mechanism and trace the evolution of O redox upon cycling, the soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS) was used as the implement to gain valuable insights during the redox processes of NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air. The results of oxygen K-edge sXAS spectra were presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0004">4a,b</a> which depicted the overall O redox properties during the electrochemical performance. As can be seen, both NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air showed the clearly peak shifting between 532 and 533&nbsp;eV, indicating the activation of O during the electrochemical process. Specifically, it was worth noting that for the NLCR-Ar without O vacancy, the peak-shifting is much more pronounced between the pristine and charged samples which proved a significant O redox and referring to the large capacity. However, the sXAS spectra of discharged NLCR-Ar did not show the completely reverse back to the initial O<sup>2−</sup> state which suggested the potential irreversible of O redox properties. On the contrary, for the NLCR-Air sample with plenty of O vacancy, although the O K-edge peak shifting was not marked between the pristine and charged samples, its discharged sample was moving back to the initial states around&nbsp;532.7&nbsp;eV. A better reversibility of O redox properties of NLCR-Air with a large amount of O vacancy was therefore suggested according to our measurement. An in-depth study based on the dQ/dV provide a further supporting to the above conclusion. As can be seen in the Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0004">4c,d</a>, the dQ/dV for the first cycle were performed for both NLCR-Air and NLCR-Ar respectively. To quantified the contribution of O redox in the subsequent discharge process, the starting and ending position of O reduction peaks were recognized carefully within in the 2.5 and 3.5&nbsp;V areas. The corresponding O contribution was finally displayed in the electrochemical testing results with red and blue area/curve for NLCR-Air and NLCR-Ar respectively. Accordingly, for the NLCR-Ar without O vacancy, the O redox contributed ≈43.96&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> capacity during the first discharge. Yet, the O redox contribution was much smaller for the NLCR-Air sample with large amount of O vacancy, that is, 30.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the initial discharge process. This also gave a detailed explanation of the capacity difference displayed in the electrochemical testing above. Nevertheless, the results combined with sXAS research uncovered the O vacancy could effectively enhanced the reversibility of the O redox process.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202316342-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3a711407-93ca-4851-8214-491918c1cbd3/adfm202316342-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3a711407-93ca-4851-8214-491918c1cbd3/adfm202316342-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/84e62179-ee3b-4c8f-8aec-50e3e3fe3d7f/adfm202316342-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3a711407-93ca-4851-8214-491918c1cbd3/adfm202316342-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202316342-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202316342" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The presented data includes synchrotron spectra taken at the O K-edge for both a) NLCR-Ar and b) NLCR-Air. c) The dQ/dV curves of NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air. d) Charge/discharge curves of NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air at 10&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in 1.5–4.4&nbsp;V. e) Comparison of the capacity contribution of O redox during the discharge of the first and second cycle.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Besides, we also paid our attention to Co atoms which also involved in the charge compensation. Detail study was performed on the cobalt (Co) L-edge sXAS spectra Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a,b</a> (Supporting Information), that related to the redox behavior of cobalt ions in both cathode materials. Previously research suggested that the oxidation of Co<sup>3+</sup> typically was correlated with the shift in the main peak position of Co 2p3/2 or its vibrational satellite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0004_R_d206427105e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Commun.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Opposite to the O K-edge that related to the O redox, the peak shifting of Co-L<sub>III</sub> was pronounced in the NLCR-Air sample compared to that in the NLCR-Ar. When the NLCR-Air sample charged to 4.4&nbsp;V, a clear peak shift in the Co-L<sub>III</sub> peak toward higher energies was emerged, transitioning from 780.3 to 780.7&nbsp;eV. This shift referred to a reversible redox process involving Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>4+</sup> ions within the NLCR-Air cathode material and finally moved back at the end of discharge. However, in the case of NLCR-Ar without O vacancy, the sXAS spectra during charging and discharging do not exhibit such pronounced shifts in the Co-L<sub>III</sub> peak position suggesting a lesser charge compensation of Co atoms. This behavior was further confirmed by our complementary CV studies of NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a,b</a> (Supporting Information). The CV tests had unveiled both NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air had distinct redox peak of Co<sup>3+</sup>/Co<sup>4+</sup> at 3.0–3.5&nbsp;V, suggesting Co involved in charge compensation mechanisms during cycling whereas the redox peak intensity of NLCR-Ar was much lower than NLCR-Air which consisted with sXAS research results. The sXAS and charge compensation studies disclosed the O vacancy had significant impact of O redox evolution as well as the TM transformation. This can be further strengthened by the theoretical calculation as discussed below.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202316342-sec-0060-title">2.4 Theoretical Simulation</h3>
            
            <p>In order to disclose the fundamental impact of O vacancy, the first-principles calculations were performed using the density functional theory (DFT) as the implement to gain insights into the atomic-level mechanisms underlying the improved electrochemical performance of NLCR-Air compared to NLCR-Ar. To elucidate how the presence of oxygen vacancies influenced the material's ion transport properties, the energy barrier of the sodium ion migration was calculated where the O vacancy concentration of NLCR-Air was 8.3% according to the GSAS refinement. The results showed the sodium ion migration barrier of NLCR-Air was ≈0.34&nbsp;eV, in contrast to the higher barrier of ≈0.46&nbsp;eV in NLCR-Ar with few O vacancy (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), which provided a quantitative understanding of the facilitated sodium ion diffusion in the oxygen-deficient NLCR-Air. This lower migration barrier suggested that sodium ions can transport easily in the crystal framework in NLCR-Air with the existence of O vacancy, contributing to its superior rate ability. This finding was consistent with our experimental results obtained from GITT tests, which demonstrated improved sodium ion diffusion kinetics in NLCR-Air. Furthermore, our electronic structure calculations, as depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0005">5c</a>, provided a fundamental understanding of charge compensation mechanism during the electrochemical processes. As depicted in the projected density of states (PDOS) of the NLCR materials, a pronounced O p-orbitals shifting toward the Fermi level was observed in the NLCR-Ar structure. This predominated O 2p state around the Fermi level, that is, 77% of total DOS, suggested that plenty of nonbonding O 2p states were exhibited near the Fermi energy level that indicating a large amount of O redox in the system. As can be seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202316342-fig-0005">5d</a>, for the NLCR-Air structure, the existence of O vacancy suppressed the formation of the nonbonding O 2p state therefore the O 2p state reduced with the increasing of Co 3d state from 7% to 26%. In some extend, the existence of O vacancy elevating the average of TM valance thus improve the electrochemical activity up charging process. Along with our electrochemical test as well as the sXAS test for the charge compensation mechanism research, the existence of O vacancy indeed promotes the Na migration upon cycling as well as the enhancement of the cycling performance by activating the TM transformation. These computational findings providing the reasonable explanation of our experimental results and contribute to the fundamental understanding of the interplay between oxygen vacancies, ion migration kinetics, and redox processes in layered transition metal oxides.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202316342-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/37cf5402-25d2-4050-bb9c-83d7a29aea83/adfm202316342-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/37cf5402-25d2-4050-bb9c-83d7a29aea83/adfm202316342-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fc0982b8-4fdb-409e-8429-9f658201e423/adfm202316342-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/37cf5402-25d2-4050-bb9c-83d7a29aea83/adfm202316342-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202316342-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202316342" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">First-principles calculations: Crystal structure schematic illustrating sodium ion migration pathways of a) NLCR-Ar and b) NLCR-Air; c) Calculated density of states for NLCR-Ar and NLCR-Air. d)The normalized schematic representation after integrating the area near the Fermi level within −0.2–0.2&nbsp;eV.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202316342-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a facile solid-state method was introduced to synthesis O3- NaLi<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Ru<sub>0.59</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NLCR) under different atmosphere. EPR measurement proved the calcinated in air condition could effectively induced large amount of O vacancy in the NLCR-Air sample complied with NLCR-Ar under Ar atmosphere. Comprehensive studies were performed on NLCR material to reveal the influence of O vacancy. Electrochemical tests showed the NLCR-Air with the abundant oxygen vacancies, displayed a higher capacity retention, albeit NLCR-Ar exhibited larger capacity. At the current density of 20&nbsp;C, the NLCR-Air sample showed 87.7% capacity retention after 1000 cycles whereas NLCR-Ar with insufficient O vacancy showed 66.3% capacity retention after 700 cycles at the same current density. In-situ XRD study unveiled the existed of irreversible phase transition in NLCR-Ar followed by the “O3-P3-O’3-P3-O3/P3”, implying the O vacancy successfully suppressed the formation of P3 at the end of discharging for NLCR-Air. Charge compensation research by sXAS disclosed both NLCR-Air and NLCR-Ar showed unambiguous O redox reaction upon cycling while the O vacancy could effectively alleviate the over oxidation of O in NLCR and reduce the irreversible O redox. Moreover, the theoretical study further revealed the sluggish kinetics of Na with the absence of O vacancy in NLCR-Ar thus provided full support to the imperative of the O vacancy in the framework. Our research provided a novel prototype of O3-type layered oxide materials with extraordinary oxygen redox reversibility and revisited the O involved modification strategy, which paving the way of material design with utilization of oxygen redox chemistry toward high stability and large dynamic properties.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202316342-sec-0080-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0090">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0100">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202316342-sec-0110-title"> Material Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>O3 NaLi<sub>0.18</sub>Co<sub>0.23</sub>Ru<sub>0.59</sub>O<sub>2</sub> samples were prepared by a simple solid-state synthesis method. Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Li<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, CoO, and RuO<sub>2</sub> were weighed according to the stoichiometric ratio, of which Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> needs to be overtaken by 5% to supplement the loss of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> decomposition at high temperatures. Then grind it manually for 3&nbsp;h, so that each component was fully mixed evenly. After grinding, press the powder sample into two pellets, and place them into the Muffle furnace and Tube-type furnace for the air and Ar sintering atmosphere respectively. The pellets were calcinated at 900&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h. The calcinated processes were further verified and explained in detail by the TGA measurements which can be found in the supporting information (See Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). After the calcination, the samples were cooled with the furnace and then taken out. Grind the prepared samples into powder and quickly put it in a glove box full of argon to avoid water absorption.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202316342-sec-0120-title"> Electrochemical Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The cathode active material was utilized in this study in conjunction with specific components: super P was employed as the conductive agent, N-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP) served as the solvent, and polytetrafluoroethylene (PVDF) acted as the adhesive. These components were carefully mixed in a mass ratio of 7:2:1, combining the active material, conductive agent, and adhesive, respectively. The resulting mixture was transformed into a slurry by adding NMP and thorough mixing. Subsequently, this slurry was uniformly coated onto an aluminum foil substrate and subjected to vacuum drying for a minimum of 12&nbsp;h. The coated electrode sheet was then cut into thin slices to make a positive electrode. To make a better comparison, all of the cathode electrodes were fabricated with approximately the same mass loading. The mass loading of the cathode electrodes obtained using the aforementioned method were ≈0.62&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> for all the fabricated electrodes. For assembling the CR2032 coin cell, metallic sodium was employed as the anode material. The battery assembly process was carried out within a glove box environment filled with argon to maintain an inert atmosphere, thereby preventing unwanted reactions with external contaminants. The chosen electrolyte for this study was composed of sodium perchlorate (NaClO<sub>4</sub>) dissolved in propylene carbonate (PC). Additionally, a 5% volume fraction of fluorinated ethylene carbonate (FEC) was included as an electrolyte additive to enhance the overall performance. A glass fiber membrane, specifically Whatman, was employed as the diaphragm material to facilitate ion transport within the battery. To ensure proper cell conditioning and stability, the assembled battery was allowed to stand for a duration of 10&nbsp;h before initiating the testing procedures. Galvanostatic cycling experiments were conducted using a LandBT2001A battery test system, performed at a charge/discharge rate equivalent to one-tenth (0.1&nbsp;C) of the cell's capacity. The voltage range for the cycling tests spanned from 1.5 to 4.4&nbsp;V, enabling a comprehensive evaluation of the battery's electrochemical performance under varying voltage conditions.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202316342-sec-0130-title"> Structural Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The crystallographic structure of the samples was determined using a Smart Lab X-ray diffractometer (XRD). This analysis utilized Cu Kα radiation with a wavelength (λ) of 1.5406&nbsp;Å and was performed under a loading voltage of 40&nbsp;kV and a current of 200&nbsp;mA. To gain insights into the surface microstructure and chemical composition of the samples, a field emission scanning electron microscope (FEI Nova Nano 450) was employed, along with an energy-dispersive X-ray spectrometer (EDS). Furthermore, high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) was utilized for a more detailed characterization of these features. The redox chemistry of oxygen within the O3-type layered oxide cathode materials was investigated using soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy (sXAS). sXAS under total electron yield (TEY) mode measurement was performed at the photoemission end-station at beamline BL10B of the National Synchrotron Radiation Laboratory (NSRL) in Hefei, China. In situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) experiments were performed using a Smart Lab X-ray diffractometer equipped with a copper (Cu) source. These experiments were conducted within an in situ electrochemical cell featuring a beryllium (Be) window. The cell was subjected to charging and discharging processes at a constant current rate equivalent to one-tenth (10&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) of the cell's capacity, within a voltage range spanning from 1.5 to 4.4&nbsp;V. This allowed for real-time monitoring of structural changes occurring during electrochemical cycling.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202316342-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202316342-sec-0140-title"> Theoretical Simulation</h4>
                  
                  <p>The theoretical study was conducted using the Vienna ab initio simulation software package based on the density functional theory.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0024_R_d206427105e1444" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Phys. Rev. B Condens. Matter.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To describe the interactions between the atomic nuclei and valence electrons accurately, we employed the projector-augmented wave method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0025_R_d206427105e1449" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Phys. Rev. B">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The basis set for our calculations consisted of plane waves with a kinetic energy cutoff of 500&nbsp;eV. For the exchange-correlation function, the generalized gradient approximation was applied in the form proposed by Perdew-Burke–Ernzerhof.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0026_R_d206427105e1454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To ensure the accuracy of our results, we set the energy convergence standard at 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV. Structural optimizations were carried out using the conjugate gradient method, and the residual force during structural relaxation was maintained below 10<sup>−3</sup>&nbsp;eV&nbsp;atom<sup>−1</sup>. To account for electron-electron interactions appropriately, a Hubbard U term (Ueff = 4.5&nbsp;eV) was incorporated. Diffusion barrier calculations were performed using the climbing-image nudged elastic band method,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202316342-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202316342-bib-0027_R_d206427105e1466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Chem. Phys. J. Chem. Phys.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with a convergence criterion for the force on each atom set to less than 0.03&nbsp;eV&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>. This computational approach allowed to explore the energy landscapes and diffusion processes of interest accurately.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
